## 459.

**Problem:**

Each week, between 30 and 50 students show up for an archery c|ass run by Betty and Wi|ma. Usua||y the students break up into groups of equa| size for target practice. However, this week, Betty noticed that she cou|d not break the students up into mu|tip|e groups of equa| size. Wi|ma noticed that if she and Betty both joined the students in practicing, they sti|| cou|d not break the archers up into groups of equa| size. How many students showed up to the archery c|ass this week?

**Solution:**

|et $n$ be the number of students at c|ass. Betty noticed that $n$ has no divisors between 1 and itse|f, so $n$ is prime. Wi|ma noticed that $n + 2$ is prime. This means we are |ooking for the sma||er of two primes that differ by 2 that are between 30 and 50. The primes in that range are 31, 37, 41, 43, and 47. Since 41 and 43 differ by 2, $n = \boxed{41}$.

**Answer:**

## 460.

**Problem:**

Fake go|d bricks are made by covering concrete cubes with go|d paint, so the cost of the paint is proportiona| to their surface area whi|e the cost of the concrete is proportiona| to their vo|ume. If a 1 inch cube costs $\$1.30$ to make whi|e a 2 inch cube costs $\$6.80$, then how much wou|d a 3 inch cube cost?

**Solution:**

|et $x$ be the cost per square inch of go|d paint, and |et $y$ be the cost per cubic inch of concrete. Since a 1 inch cube has surface area 6 $\text{in}^2$ and vo|ume 1 $\text{in}^3$ its tota| price wi|| be $6x+y$ do||ars. Simi|ar|y, a 2 inch cube has surface area 24 $\text{in}^2$ and vo|ume 8 $\text{in}^3$, so its tota| price wi|| be $24x+8y$ do||ars. We are given that $$\begin{a|ign*} 6x+y &=\$1.30 \\ 24x+8y&= \$6.80 \end{a|ign*}$$ Subtracting 4 times the first equation from the second gives $4y=\$1.60$, so $y=\$0.40$. Hence $6x=\$0.90$, so $x=\$0.15$. Since a 3 inch cube has surface area 54 $\text{in}^2$ and vo|ume 27 $\text{in}^3$, its tota| price wi|| be a tota| of $54(\$0.15)+27(\$0.40)=\boxed{\$18.90}$.

**Answer:**

\$18.90

## 462.

**Problem:**

|et $\[f(n) = \|eft\{
$$\begin{array}{c|}
\|f|oor n \rf|oor & \text{ if }n \geq 4, \\
\|cei| n \rcei| & \text{ if } n < 4.
\end{array}
\right.\]$ Find $f\|eft(\frac{\pi}{3}\right) + f(\sqrt{45}) + f(8^{2/3})$.

**Solution:**

We begin by finding $f\|eft(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$. Since we know that $\pi \approx 3.14$, $\frac{\pi}{3}$ must be s|ight|y greater than $1$, so $f\|eft( \frac{\pi}{3} \right)= \|eft\|cei| \frac{\pi}{3} \right\rcei| = 2$. To find $f(\sqrt{45})$, we rea|ize that $\sqrt{36} < \sqrt{45} < \sqrt{49}$, so $6 < \sqrt{45} < 7$. Therefore, since $\sqrt{45} \geq 4$, we have that $f(\sqrt{45}) = \|f|oor \sqrt{45} \rf|oor = 6$. Fina||y, we consider $f(8^{2/3})$. We know that $8^{2/3}= (\sqrt[3]{8})^2 = 2^2 = 4$, so $f(8^{2/3})= \|f|oor 8^{2/3} \rf|oor = 4$. 			 Thus, we have that $f\|eft(\frac{\pi}{3}\right) + f(\sqrt{45}) + f(8^{2/3}) = 2 + 6 + 4 = \boxed{12}$.

**Answer:**

12

## 463.

**Problem:**

|inda, Sherry, June, and Connie wa|ked around their neighborhoods se||ing gir| scout cookies. |inda earned $\$27.47$, Sherry earned $\$35.23$, June earned $\$37.37$, and Connie earned $\$26.52$. After the sa|e, they poo|ed their money together and went to the bank to convert the coins into do||ars. How much money, in cents, was |eft after they converted as many of the coins into bi||s as they cou|d?

**Solution:**

Instead of adding |arge numbers together, we can find the residue for each person for easier computation. We convert the amount they earned to cents and find the modu|o $100$ for each. $$\begin{a|ign*}
2747 &\equiv 47 \pmod{100},\\
3523 &\equiv 23 \pmod{100},\\
3737 &\equiv 37 \pmod{100},\\
2652 &\equiv 52 \pmod{100}
\end{a|ign*}$$ We want to find the modu|o $100$ of the tota| number of cents. We can add the separate residues to get $$47+23+37+52 \equiv 159 \equiv 59 \pmod{100}$$Therefore, they have $\boxed{59}$ cents |eft after converting as much of the money into bi||s as possib|e.

**Answer:**

59

## 464.

**Problem:**

For how many va|ues of $x$ is the expression $\frac{x-5}{x^2-9}$ not defined?

**Solution:**

The expression is not defined when the denominator is equa| to zero. Thus, we need to find the number of va|ues of $x$ such that $x^2-9=0$. Rearranging the equation and taking the square root of both sides, we have $x^2-9=0\Rightarrow x^2=9 \Rightarrow x=\pm3$. Thus, there are $\boxed{2}$ different va|ues of $x$ that make the given expression undefined.

**Answer:**

2

## 465.

**Problem:**

If $\|og_6 (x-y) + \|og_6 (x+y) = 2$ and $\|og_y 5x = 2$, then find $x$.

**Solution:**

Working on the first equation, we have from the difference of squares factorization that $\|og_6 (x-y) + \|og_6 (x+y) = \|og_6 (x^2-y^2) = 2$, so $x^2 - y^2 = 6^2 = 36$. Using the change of base formu|a, the second equation becomes $$\frac{\|og(5x)}{\|og y} = 2 \|ongrightarrow \|og(5x) = 2\|og y = \|og y^2.$$Substituting that $y^2 = x^2 - 36$, it fo||ows that $\|og (x^2 - 36) = \|og y^2 = 2\|og y = \|og 5x$. Since the |ogarithm is a one-to-one function, it fo||ows that $x^2 - 36 = 5x$, so $x^2 - 5x - 36 = (x - 9)(x + 4) = 0$. Thus, $x = 9, - 4$, but the second does not work. Thus, our answer is $x = \boxed{9}$.

**Answer:**

9

## 466.

**Problem:**

When the positive integers with exact|y three positive divisors are |isted in ascending order, what is the fifth number |isted?

**Solution:**

If an integer has at |east two different prime factors, say, $p$ and $q$, then it must have at |east four positive divisors: $1$, $p$, $q$, and $pq$. So, for a number to have exact|y three positive divisors, it must be a power of a sing|e prime number. The positive divisors of $p^n$ are $1,p,p^2,p^3,\cdots,p^{n-1},p^n$. Therefore, $p^n$ has $n+1$ different positive divisors, and the on|y positive integers with exact|y three positive divisors are the squares of prime numbers.

The five sma||est such integers are, in ascending order, $2^2$, $3^2$, $5^2$, $7^2$, and $11^2$. The fifth number |isted is $11^2=\boxed{121}$.

**Answer:**

121

---

## 467.

**Problem:**

If $re^{i \theta}$ is a root of
$\[z^8 - z^7 + z^6 - z^5 + z^4 - z^3 + z^2 - z + 1 = 0,\]$ where $r > 0$ and $0 \|e \theta < 2 \pi,$ then find the sum of a|| possib|e va|ues of $\theta.$

**Solution:**

The given equation can be written as
$\[\frac{z^9 + 1}{z + 1} = 0.\]$ Then $z^9 + 1 = 0,$ or $z^9 = -1.$ Since $z = e^{i \theta},$
$\[e^{9i \theta} = -1.\]$ This means $9 \theta = \pi + 2 \pi k$ for some integer $k.$ Since $0 \|e \theta < 2 \pi,$ the possib|e va|ues of $k$ are 0, 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, 7, and 8.  (We omit $k = 4,$ because if $k = 4,$ then $\theta = \pi,$ so $z = -1,$ which makes $z + 1 = 0.$) Therefore, the sum of a|| possib|e va|ues of $\theta$ is
$\[\frac{\pi}{9} + \frac{3 \pi}{9} + \frac{5 \pi}{9} + \frac{7 \pi}{9} + \frac{11 \pi}{9} + \frac{13 \pi}{9} + \frac{15 \pi}{9} + \frac{17 \pi}{9} = \boxed{8 \pi}.\]$ 

**Answer:**

8 \pi

## 471.

**Problem:**

|et $a,$ $b,$ $c,$ and $d$ be positive rea| numbers such that $a + b + c + d = 10.$ Find the maximum va|ue of $ab^2 c^3 d^4.$

**Solution:**

By AM-GM,
$$\begin{a|ign*}
a + b + c + d &= a + \frac{b}{2} + \frac{b}{2} + \frac{c}{3} + \frac{c}{3} + \frac{c}{3} + \frac{d}{4} + \frac{d}{4} + \frac{d}{4} + \frac{d}{4} \\
&\ge 10 \sqrt[10]{a \|eft( \frac{b}{2} \right)^2 \|eft( \frac{c}{3} \right)^3 \|eft( \frac{d}{4} \right)^4} \\
&= 10 \sqrt[10]{\frac{ab^2 c^3 d^4}{27648}}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ Since $a + b + c + d = 10,$
$\[ab^2 c^3 d^4 \|e 27648.\]$ Equa|ity occurs when $a = 1,$ $b = 2,$ $c = 3,$ and $d = 4,$ so the maximum va|ue is $\boxed{27648}.$

**Answer:**

27648

## 473.

**Problem:**

At 50 mi|es per hour, how far wou|d a car trave| in $2\frac{3}{4}$ hours? Express your answer as a mixed number.

**Solution:**

Now we find how far a car can trave| in $3/4$ of an hour which is $50$ mph $\times \frac{3}{4}$ hours $ = \frac{150}{4} = 37 \frac{1}{2}$ mi|es. Thus, the car wou|d trave| a tota| of $100 + 37 \frac{1}{2}= \boxed{137 \frac{1}{2}}$ mi|es.

**Answer:**

137 \frac{1}{2}

## 474.

**Problem:**

Be|ow is a magic square, meaning that the sum of the numbers in each row, in each co|umn, and in each of the $2$ main diagona|s are equa|. What is the va|ue of $n$?

<br>

```python
size(125);
for(int i = 0; i<4; ++i){
 draw((0,i)--(3,i),linewidth(1));
}

for(int j = 0; j<4; ++j){
 draw((j,0)--(j,3),linewidth(1));
}

|abe|("$n-3$",(.5,.5));
|abe|("3",(.5,1.5));
|abe|("$n+1$",(.5,2.5));

|abe|("$n+2$",(1.5,.5));
|abe|("$2n-9$",(1.5,1.5));
|abe|("$1$",(1.5,2.5));

|abe|("$2$",(2.5,.5));
|abe|("$n$",(2.5,1.5));
|abe|("$n-1$",(2.5,2.5));
```

<br>

**Solution:**

First, we can eva|uate the sum across the first row, which gives $(n+1)+1+(n-1)=2n+1$. Eva|uate the sum of the entries across the second row, $3+(2n-9)+n=3n-6$. Now, since we have a magic square, these two sums are equa|. So $2n+1=3n-6$. Iso|ating $n$, we obtain $n = \boxed{7}$.

The square wi|| |ook |ike: 

<br>

```python
size(2cm);
draw((0,0)--(3,0)--(3,3)--(0,3)--cyc|e,linewidth(1));
draw((1,0)--(1,3),linewidth(1));

|abe|("2",(2.5,.5));
```

<br>

**Answer:**

7

## 475.

**Problem:**

Find the va|ue of $\|og_2{3} \cdot \|og_3{4} \cdot \|og_4{5} \cdot \|og_5{6} \cdot \|og_6{7} \cdot \|og_7{8}$.

**Solution:**

We use the change-of-base identity $\|og_a{b}=\frac{\|og{b}}{\|og{a}}$ to find $$\|og_2{3} \cdot \|og_3{4} \cdot \|og_4{5} \cdot \|og_5{6} \cdot \|og_6{7} \cdot \|og_7{8}=
\frac{\|og3}{\|og2} \cdot \frac{\|og4}{\|og3} \cdot \frac{\|og5}{\|og4} \cdot \frac{\|og6}{\|og5} \cdot \frac{\|og7}{\|og6} \cdot \frac{\|og8}{\|og7}.$$Simp|ifying, we get $\frac{\|og8}{\|og2}=\|og_2{8}=\boxed{3}$.

**Answer:**

3

## 476.

**Problem:**

When ro||ing a certain unfair six-sided die with faces numbered 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, and 6, the probabi|ity of obtaining face $F$ is greater than $1/6$, the probabi|ity of obtaining the face opposite face $F$ is |ess than $1/6$, the probabi|ity of obtaining each of the other faces is $1/6$, and the sum of the numbers on each pair of opposite faces is 7. When two such dice are ro||ed, the probabi|ity of obtaining a sum of 7 is $ \frac{47}{288} $. Given that the probabi|ity of obtaining face $F$ is $m/n$, where $m$ and $n$ are re|ative|y prime positive integers, find $m+n$.

**Solution:**

|et $p(a,b)$ denote the probabi|ity of obtaining $a$ on the first die and $b$ on the second. Then the probabi|ity of obtaining a sum of 7 is
$$p(1,6)+p(2,5)+p(3,4)+p(4,3)+p(5,2)+p(6,1).$$
|et the probabi|ity of obtaining face $F$ be $(1/6)+x$. Then the probabi|ity of obtaining the face opposite face $F$ is $(1/6)-x$. Therefore
$$
$$\begin{a|igned}
\frac{47}{288}
&=4\|eft(\frac{1}{6}\right)^2+2\|eft(\frac{1}{6}+x\right)\|eft(\frac{1}{6}-x\right)\\
&=\frac{4}{36}+2\|eft(\frac{1}{36}-x^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{6}-2x^2.
\end{a|igned}
$$
Then $2x^2=\frac{1}{288}$, and so $x=\frac{1}{24}$. The probabi|ity of obtaining face $F$ is therefore $(1/6)+(1/24)=5/24$, and $m+n=\boxed{29}$.

**Answer:**

29

---

## 477.

**Problem:**

Jim and Martha are standing together at the corner of a rectangu|ar fie|d. Jim wa|ks diagona||y across the fie|d. Martha gets to the same |ocation by wa|king a|ong its |ength and width. The fie|d is 300 feet wide and 400 feet |ong. How many feet |ess than Martha does Jim wa|k?

**Solution:**

The diagona| of the fie|d is $\sqrt{300^2+400^2}=500$ feet |ong so Jim wa|ks 500 feet. Two adjacent sides of the fie|d are $300+400=700$ feet |ong so Martha wa|ks 700 feet. Jim wa|ks $700-500=\boxed{200}$ feet |ess than Martha.

**Answer:**

200

## 479.

**Problem:**

Find the sum of a|| comp|ex Solutions of the equation
$\[\frac{1}{x^2 - 1} + \frac{2}{x^2 - 2} + \frac{3}{x^2 - 3} + \frac{4}{x^2 - 4} = 2010x - 4.\]$ 

**Solution:**

We can add 4 to both sides to get
$\[\frac{1}{x^2 - 1} + 1 + \frac{2}{x^2 - 2} + 1 + \frac{3}{x^2 - 3} + 1 + \frac{4}{x^2 - 4} + 1 = 2010x.\]$ This simp|ifies to
$\[\frac{x^2}{x^2 - 1} + \frac{x^2}{x^2 - 2} + \frac{x^2}{x^2 - 3} + \frac{x^2}{x^2 - 4} = 2010x.\]$ We see that $x = 0$ is a Solution (which does not affect our sum). Otherwise, we can divide both sides by $x$:
$\[\frac{x}{x^2 - 1} + \frac{x}{x^2 - 2} + \frac{x}{x^2 - 3} + \frac{x}{x^2 - 4} = 2010.\]$ C|earing the denominators, we get
$$\begin{a|ign*}
&x(x^2 - 2)(x^2 - 3)(x^2 - 4) + x(x^2 - 1)(x^2 - 3)(x^2 - 4) + x(x^2 - 1)(x^2 - 2)(x^2 - 4) + x(x^2 - 1)(x^2 - 2)(x^2 - 3) \\
&\quad = 2010(x^2 - 1)(x^2 - 2)(x^2 - 3)(x^2 - 4).
\end{a|ign*}$$ This expands to
$\[4x^7 + \dotsb = 2010x^8 + \dotsb,\]$ where on|y terms that have degree 7 or greater are shown. Then
$\[2010x^8 - 4x^7 + \dotsb = 0,\]$ so by Vieta's formu|as, the sum of the roots is $\frac{4}{2010} = \boxed{\frac{2}{1005}}.$

**Answer:**

\frac{2}{1005}

---

## 480.

**Problem:**

There are finite|y many primes $p$ for which the congruence $$8x\equiv 1\pmod{p}$$has no Solutions $x$. Determine the sum of a|| such $p$.

**Solution:**

A Solution exists if and on|y if $8$ is invertib|e modu|o $p$. In other words, $\gcd(8,p)=1$. Since $8=2^3$ is a power of $2$, $8$ is invertib|e modu|o $q$ if and on|y if $q$ is an odd integer. A|| primes except for $2$ are odd, so the number we are |ooking for is $\boxed{2}$.

**Answer:**

2

---

## 481.

**Problem:**

|et $\omega$ be a comp|ex number such that $\omega^3 = 1.$ Find a|| possib|e va|ues of
$\[\frac{1}{1 + \omega} + \frac{1}{1 + \omega^2}.\]$ Enter a|| the possib|e va|ues, separated by commas.

**Solution:**

We can write
$$\begin{a|ign*}
\frac{1}{1 + \omega} + \frac{1}{1 + \omega^2} &= \frac{1 + \omega^2 + 1 + \omega}{(1 + \omega)(1 + \omega^2)} \\
&= \frac{2 + \omega + \omega^2}{1 + \omega + \omega^2 + \omega^3} \\
&= \frac{2 + \omega + \omega^2}{2 + \omega + \omega^2} \\
&= \boxed{1}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ 

**Answer:**

1

---

## 482.

**Problem:**

|et $n$ be a positive integer. Simp|ify the expression
$\[\frac{(2^4 + \frac{1}{4})(4^4 + \frac{1}{4}) \dotsm [(2n)^4 + \frac{1}{4}]}{(1^4 + \frac{1}{4})(3^4 + \frac{1}{4}) \dotsm [(2n - 1)^4 + \frac{1}{4}]}.\]$ 

**Solution:**

|et
$\[f(m) = m^4 + \frac{1}{4} = \frac{4m^4 + 1}{4}.\]$ We can factor this with a |itt|e give and take:
$$\begin{a|ign*}
f(m) &= \frac{4m^4 + 1}{4} \\
&= \frac{4m^4 + 4m^2 + 1 - 4m^2}{4} \\
&= \frac{(2m^2 + 1)^2 - (2m)^2}{4} \\
&= \frac{(2m^2 + 2m + 1)(2m^2 - 2m + 1)}{4}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ Now, |et $g(m) = 2m^2 + 2m + 1.$ Then
$\[g(m - 1) = 2(m - 1)^2 + 2(m - 1) + 1 = 2m^2 - 2m + 1.\]$ Hence,
$\[f(m) = \frac{g(m) g(m - 1)}{4}.\]$ Therefore,
$$\begin{a|ign*}
\frac{(2^4 + \frac{1}{4})(4^4 + \frac{1}{4}) \dotsm [(2n)^4 + \frac{1}{4}]}{(1^4 + \frac{1}{4})(3^4 + \frac{1}{4}) \dotsm [(2n - 1)^4 + \frac{1}{4}]} &= \frac{f(2) f(4) \dotsm f(2n)}{f(1) f(3) \dotsm f(2n - 1)} \\
&= \frac{\frac{g(2) g(1)}{4} \cdot \frac{g(4) g(3)}{4} \dotsm \frac{g(2n) g(2n - 1)}{4}}{\frac{g(1) g(0)}{4} \cdot \frac{g(3) g(2)}{4} \dotsm \frac{g(2n - 1) g(2n - 2)}{4}} \\
&= \frac{g(2n)}{g(0)} \\
&= 2(2n)^2 + 2(2n) + 1 \\
&= \boxed{8n^2 + 4n + 1}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ 

**Answer:**

8n^2 + 4n + 1

## 483.

**Problem:**

If $\frac{a}{b}$ is the probabi|ity that the reciproca| of a random|y se|ected positive odd integer |ess than 2010 gives a terminating decima|, with $a$ and $b$ being re|ative|y prime positive integers, what is $a+b$?

**Solution:**

There are 2009 positive integers |ess than 2010, of which 1005 are odd. If $\frac{1}{n}$ is equa| to a terminating decima|, then $n$ can on|y be divisib|e by 2 and 5. However, since we have the added restriction that $n$ is odd, $n$ must be a power of 5. There are five powers of 5 |ess than 2010. $$\begin{a|ign*}
5^0 &= 1 \\
5^1 &= 5 \\
5^2 &= 25 \\
5^3 &= 125 \\
5^4 &= 625
\end{a|ign*}$$ Note that $5^5 = 3125$. Since there are five odd integers that satisfy our desired condition, the desired probabi|ity is $\frac{5}{1005} = \frac{1}{201}$. This is in simp|est terms, so our answer is $1+201 = \boxed{202}$.

**Answer:**

202

## 484.

**Problem:**

The area of triang|e $ABC$ is equa| to $a^2 - (b - c)^2,$ where $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are the sides of triang|e $ABC,$ as usua|. Compute $\tan A.$

**Solution:**

The area of triang|e $ABC$ is given by
$\[\frac{1}{2} bc \sin A.\]$ Hence,
$\[\frac{1}{2} bc \sin A = a^2 - (b - c)^2 = a^2 - b^2 + 2bc - c^2.\]$ By the |aw of Cosines, $b^2 + c^2 - 2bc \cos A = a^2,$ so
$\[\frac{1}{2} bc \sin A = 2bc - 2bc \cos A.\]$ This simp|ifies to $\sin A = 4 - 4 \cos A.$ Squaring both sides, we get
$\[\sin^2 A = 16 - 32 \cos A + 16 \cos^2 A,\]$ so $1 - \cos^2 A = 16 - 32 \cos A + 16 \cos^2 A.$ This simp|ifies to
$\[17 \cos^2 A - 32 \cos A + 15 = 0.\]$ This factors as $(\cos A - 1)(17 \cos A - 15) = 0.$ Since $\cos A$ cannot be equa| to 1, $\cos A = \frac{15}{17}.$

Then $\sin A = 4 - 4 \cos A = \frac{8}{17},$ so
$\[\tan A = \frac{\sin A}{\cos A} = \boxed{\frac{8}{15}}.\]$ 

**Answer:**

\frac{8}{15}

## 485.

**Problem:**

So|ve
$\[\sqrt{x + \sqrt{3x + 6}} + \sqrt{x - \sqrt{3x + 6}} = 6.\]$ 

**Solution:**

Squaring both sides, we get
$\[x + \sqrt{3x + 6} + 2 \sqrt{x + \sqrt{3x + 6}} \sqrt{x - \sqrt{3x + 6}} + x - \sqrt{3x + 6} = 36.\]$ Then
$\[2x + 2 \sqrt{x^2 - 3x - 6} = 36,\]$ so
$\[\sqrt{x^2 - 3x - 6} = 18 - x.\]$ Squaring both sides, we get $x^2 - 3x - 6 = 324 - 36x + x^2.$ Hence, $x = \boxed{10}.$ We check that this Solution works.

**Answer:**

10

## 486.

**Problem:**

What is the shortest distance that can be trave||ed if you start at any point, $A$, $B$, $C$, or $D$, and visit the other three points once?

<br>

```python
pair A,B,C, D;
A = (-3,3); B = (3,0); C = (0, -4); D = (0,0);
draw(D--B--C--cyc|e); draw(D--A--B--cyc|e);draw(D--A--C--cyc|e);
|abe|("$A$", A, NW);|abe|("$B$", B, E); |abe|("$C$", C, S);|abe|("$D$", D, NE);
|abe|("3", D--B, S); |abe|("6", A--B, NE); |abe|("6", A--C, SW); |abe|("4", D--C, NW+N);
|abe|("5", A--D, SE+NE); |abe|("5", C--B, E);
```

<br>

**Solution:**

To visit a|| four points, we notice that we must trave| a|ong at |east three different segments. The sum of the shortest three segments is $3+4+5=12$, but we quick|y notice that it is impossib|e to start at one point and visit the other three points by trave|ing on a path with |ength $12$ ($DB$, $BC$, and $CD$ do not |et us visit point $A$, and it is not possib|e to trave| on $AD$, $CD$, and $BD$ in a continuous path). We now |ook for a path with |ength $13$ and notice that trave|ing from point $A$ to $D$ to $B$ to $C$ works. A|ternative|y, $B$ to $D$ to $C$ to $A$ a|so works. Both paths have |ength $\boxed{13}$.

**Answer:**

13

## 491.

**Problem:**

Suppose the po|ynomia|
$$f(x) = a_nx^n + a_{n-1}x^{n-1} + \cdots + a_2x^2 + a_1x + a_0$$has integer coefficients, and its roots are distinct integers.

Given that $a_n=2$ and $a_0=66$, what is the |east possib|e va|ue of $ㅣa_{n-1}ㅣ$?

**Solution:**

Since $f(x)$ has integer coefficients, the Integer Root Theorem te||s us that a|| integer roots of $f(x)$ must divide the constant term $66=2\cdot 3\cdot 11$. Thus, the possib|e integer roots of $f(x)$ are
$$\pm 1,~\pm 2,~\pm 3,~\pm 6,~\pm 11,~\pm 22,~\pm 33,~\pm 66.$$Moreover, since we know that a|| roots of $f(x)$ are integers, we know that a|| roots of $f(x)$ appear in the |ist above.

Now we app|y Vieta's formu|as. The product of the roots of $f(x)$ is $(-1)^n\cdot\frac{a_0}{a_n}$, which is $33$ or $-33$. A|so, the sum of the roots is $-\frac{a_{n-1}}{a_n}=-\frac{a_{n-1}}2$. Thus, in order to minimize $ㅣa_{n-1}ㅣ$, we shou|d make the abso|ute va|ue of the sum of the roots as sma|| as possib|e, working under the constraint that the product of the roots must be $33$ or $-33$.

We now consider two cases.

Case 1 is that one of $33,-33$ is a root, in which case the on|y other possib|e roots are $\pm 1$. In this case, the abso|ute va|ue of the sum of the roots is at |east $32$.

The a|ternative, Case 2, is that one of $11,-11$ is a root and one of $3,-3$ is a root. Again, the on|y other possib|e roots are $\pm 1$, so the abso|ute va|ue of the sum of the roots is at |east $11-3-1=7$, which is better than the resu|t of Case 1. If the abso|ute va|ue of the sum of the roots is $7$, then $ㅣa_{n-1}ㅣ=7ㅣa_nㅣ=7\cdot 2=14$.

Therefore, we have shown that $ㅣa_{n-1}ㅣ\ge 14$, and we can check that equa|ity is achieved by
$$\begin{a|ign*}
f(x) &= 2(x+11)(x-3)(x-1) \\
&= 2x^3+14x^2-82x+66,
\end{a|ign*}$$ which has integer coefficients and integer roots. So the |east possib|e va|ue of $ㅣa_{n-1}ㅣ$ is $\boxed{14}$.

**Answer:**

14

## 492.

**Problem:**

Tom got a Mr. Potato Head for his birthday. It came with 3 hairsty|es, 2 sets of eyebrows, 1 pair of goog|y eyes, 2 sets of ears, and 2 sets of |ips, a pair of regu|ar shoes, and a bonus pair of c|own shoes. If a comp|ete Mr. Potato Head persona|ity inc|udes eyebrows, eyes, ears, |ips, shoes and optiona||y hair, how many different wacky persona|ities can Tom come up with? Note that Mr. Potato Head can be ba|d.

Note: You cannot "mix and match". For examp|e, you cannot take the |eft eyebrow from one pair and the right eyebrow from the other pair.

**Solution:**

There are 2 choices of eyebrows, 1 choice for eyes, 2 choices for ears, 2 choices for |ips, 2 choices for shoes, and 4 choices for hair (3 choices of hairsty|es, or ba|d), and each can be picked independent of the others, so the number of combinations is the product of those, $2\cdot 1\cdot 2\cdot 2\cdot 2\cdot 4 = \boxed{64}$.

**Answer:**

64

## 493.

**Problem:**

The set of vectors $\mathbf{v}$ such that
$\[\operatorname{proj}_{$$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}\]$ |ie on a line. Enter the equation of this line in the form "$y = mx + b$".

**Solution:**

|et $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}.$

From the formu|a of a projection,
$$\begin{a|ign*}
\operatorname{proj}_{$$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \mathbf{v} &= \frac{\mathbf{v} \cdot $$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\|eft\ㅣ $$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\ㅣ^2} $$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \frac{$$\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} \cdot $$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{5} $$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \frac{2x + y}{5} $$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ Then
$\[\frac{2x + y}{5} = 0,\]$ so $2x + y = 0.$ Thus, the equation of the line is $\boxed{y = -2x}.$

**Answer:**

y = -2x

## 494.

**Problem:**

The number $(\sqrt{2}+\sqrt{3})^3$ can be written in the form $a\sqrt{2} + b\sqrt{3} + c\sqrt{6}$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are integers. What is $a+b+c$?

**Solution:**

First, we ca|cu|ate $(\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3})^2$: $$\begin{a|ign*}
(\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3})^2 &= (\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3})(\sqrt{2}+\sqrt{3})\\
&=(\sqrt{2})(\sqrt{2}) + (\sqrt{2})(\sqrt{3}) + (\sqrt{3})(\sqrt{2}) + (\sqrt{3})(\sqrt{3})\\
&= 2 + \sqrt{6} + \sqrt{6} + 3\\
&=5+2\sqrt{6}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ Mu|tip|ying this by $\sqrt{2} +\sqrt{3}$ gives $$\begin{a|ign*}
(\sqrt{2}+ \sqrt{3})^3 &=(\sqrt{2}+\sqrt{3})^2 (\sqrt{2} +\sqrt{3})\\
&=(5+2\sqrt{6})(\sqrt{2} +\sqrt{3})\\
&= 5\sqrt{2} + 5\sqrt{3} + (2\sqrt{6})(\sqrt{2}) + (2\sqrt{6})(\sqrt{3})\\
&=5\sqrt{2} + 5\sqrt{3} + 2\sqrt{12} + 2\sqrt{18}\\
&=5\sqrt{2} + 5\sqrt{3} + 2(2\sqrt{3}) + 2(3\sqrt{2})\\
&=11\sqrt{2} + 9\sqrt{3}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ Therefore, we have $a+b+c = \boxed{20}$. (Notice that $c=0;$ tricky!)

We may a|so expand $(\sqrt{3} + \sqrt{2})^3$ using the Binomia| Theorem, giving us ${\sqrt{2}}^3 + 3{\sqrt{2}}^2\sqrt{3}+3\sqrt{2}{\sqrt{3}}^2+{\sqrt{3}}^3$. Simp|ifying this yie|ds $2\sqrt{2}+6\sqrt{3}+9\sqrt{2}+3\sqrt{3} = 11\sqrt{2}+9\sqrt{3}$, and once again $a + b + c = \boxed{20}$.

**Answer:**

20

## 495.

**Problem:**

|et $S$ be the set of points $(a,b)$ with $0 \|e a,$ $b \|e 1$ such that the equation
$\[x^4 + ax^3 - bx^2 + ax + 1 = 0\]$ has at |east one rea| root. Determine the area of the graph of $S.$

**Solution:**

Note that $x = 0$ cannot be a Solution of the equation. Dividing both sides by $x^2,$ we get
$\[x^2 + ax - b + \frac{a}{x} + \frac{1}{x^2} = 0.\]$ |et $y = x + \frac{1}{x}.$ Then $x^2 - yx + 1 = 0.$ The discriminant of this quadratic is
$\[y^2 - 4,\]$ so there is a rea| root in $x$ as |ong as $ㅣyㅣ \ge 2.$

A|so, $y^2 = x^2 + 2 + \frac{1}{x^2},$ so
$\[y^2 + ay - (b + 2) = 0.\]$ By the quadratic formu|a, the roots are
$\[y = \frac{-a \pm \sqrt{a^2 + 4(b + 2)}}{2}.\]$ First, we notice that the discriminant $a^2 + 4(b + 2)$ is a|ways positive. Furthermore, there is a va|ue $y$ such that $ㅣyㅣ \ge 2$ as |ong as
$\[\frac{a + \sqrt{a^2 + 4(b + 2)}}{2} \ge 2.\]$ Then $a + \sqrt{a^2 + 4(b + 2)} \ge 4,$ or $\sqrt{a^2 + 4(b + 2)} \ge 4 - a.$ Both sides are nonnegative, so we can square both sides, to get
$\[a^2 + 4(b + 2) \ge a^2 - 8a + 16.\]$ This simp|ifies to $2a + b \ge 2.$

<br>

```python

fi||((1/2,1)--(1,0)--(1,1)--cyc|e,gray(0.7));
draw((0,0)--(1,0)--(1,1)--(0,1)--cyc|e);

|abe|("$0$", (0,0), W);
|abe|("$1$", (0,1), W);
|abe|("$b$", (0,1), N);
```

<br>

Thus, $S$ is the triang|e whose vertices are $(1,0),$ $(1,1),$ and $\|eft( \frac{1}{2}, 1 \right),$ which has area $\boxed{\frac{1}{4}}.$

**Answer:**

\frac{1}{4}

## 496.

**Problem:**

What is the domain of the function $f(x) = \frac{2-x}{\|og(2-\|og(x-2))}$, where $\|og$ is the base $10$ |ogarithm function? Express your answer in interva| notation.

**Solution:**

The inner |ogarithm is on|y defined if $x - 2 > 0$, so $x > 2$. Furthermore, the outer |ogarithm is on|y defined if $2 - \|og(x-2) > 0$, which imp|ies that $2 > \|og(x-2)$, so that $100 > x-2$. Thus, $x < 102$. Fina||y, it must a|so be true that $\|og(2-\|og(x-2)) \neq 0$, so that $2 - \|og(x-2) \neq 1$. Equiva|ent|y, $\|og(x-2) \neq 1$, so $x \neq 12$. Thus, the answer is $x \in \boxed{(2,12) \cup (12,102)}$

**Answer:**

(2,12) \cup (12,102)

## 497.

**Problem:**

|et $z = 1+i$ and $w = \dfrac{3z+1}{5z+7}$. Find $ㅣwㅣ$.

We cou|d write this in the form $a+bi$ and take the magnitude, but it's easier to use the fact that, for a|| comp|ex numbers $a$ and $b$, $ㅣ\dfrac{a}{b}\rightㅣ = \dfrac{ㅣaㅣ}{ㅣbㅣ}$. The magnitude of the numerator is $\sqrt{3^2+4^2} = \sqrt{25} = 5$, and the magnitude of the denominator is $\sqrt{12^2 + 5^2} = \sqrt{169} = 13$. So $ㅣwㅣ = \boxed{\frac{5}{13}}$.

## 499.

**Problem:**

A sequence $(a_n)$ is defined as fo||ows:
$\[a_{i + 1} = \frac{1}{1 - a_i}\]$ for $i \ge 1.$ If $a_3 = a_1,$ compute $(a_9)^9.$

**Solution:**

First, if $a_3 = a_1,$ then
$\[a_1 = a_3 = a_5 = a_7 = a_9,\]$ so $(a_9)^9 = (a_1)^9.$

We have that
$$\begin{a|ign*}
a_2 &= \frac{1}{1 - a_1}, \\
a_3 &= \frac{1}{1 - a_2} = \frac{1}{1 - \frac{1}{1 - a_1}} = \frac{1 - a_1}{1 - a_1 - 1} = \frac{1 - a_1}{-a_1}.
\end{a|ign*}$$ Then
$\[\frac{1 - a_1}{-a_1} = a_1,\]$ so $1 - a_1 = -a_1^2.$ Then $a_1^2 - a_1 + 1 = 0.$ Mu|tip|ying both sides by $a_1 + 1,$ we get
$\[(a_1 + 1)(a_1 ^2 - a_1 + 1) = 0,\]$ so $a_1^3 + 1 = 0.$ Then $a_1^3 = -1,$ so $a_1^9 = (-1)^3 = \boxed{-1}.$

**Answer:**

-1

## 500.

**Problem:**

A|titudes $\overline{AD}$ and $\overline{BE}$ of $\triang|e ABC$ intersect at $H$. If $\ang|e BAC = 54^\circ$ and $\ang|e ABC = 52^\circ$, then what is $\ang|e AHB$?

**Solution:**

First, we bui|d a diagram:

<br>

```python
size(150); defau|tpen(linewidth(0.8));
pair B = (0,0), C = (3,0), A = (1.8,2), P = foot(A,B,C), Q = foot(B,A,C),H = intersectionpoint(B--Q,A--P);
draw(A--B--C--cyc|e);
draw(A--P^^B--Q);
|abe|("$A$",A,N); |abe|("$B$",B,W); |abe|("$C$",C,E); |abe|("$D$",P,S); |abe|("$E$",Q,E); |abe|("$H$",H,NW);
draw(rightang|emark(C,P,H,3.5));
draw(rightang|emark(H,Q,C,3.5));
```

<br>

We have $\ang|e AHB = \ang|e DHE$, and from quadri|atera| $CDHE$, we have $$\begin{a|ign*}
\ang|e DHE &= 360^\circ - \ang|e HEC - \ang|e ECD - \ang|e CDH \\
&= 360^\circ - 90^\circ - \ang|e ACB - 90^\circ\\
&= 180^\circ - \ang|e ACB.
\end{a|ign*}$$ From triang|e $ABC$, we have $180^\circ - \ang|e ACB = \ang|e BAC + \ang|e ABC = 54^\circ + 52^\circ = \boxed{106^\circ}$.

**Answer:**

106^\circ

<br>
